      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       , in various dialects, is the most widely spoken
       <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">
        language
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        United Kingdom
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       however there are a number of regional languages also spoken. There are 11 indigenous languages spoken across the
       <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">
        British Isles
       </a>
       : 5
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">
        Celtic
       </a>
       , 3
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
        Germanic
       </a>
       , and 3
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance
       </a>
       . There are also many immigrant languages spoken in the British Isles, mainly within inner city areas; these languages are mainly from South Asia and Eastern Europe.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">
         de facto
        </a>
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">
        official language
       </a>
       of the United Kingdom is English,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keyfacts_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-keyfacts-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-direct.gov.uk_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-direct.gov.uk-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which is spoken by approximately 59.8 million residents, or 98% of the population, over the age of three.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnglishCensus_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-EnglishCensus-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbclang_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbclang-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An estimated 700,000 people speak
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
        Welsh
       </a>
       in the UK,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WelshStatOverview_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-WelshStatOverview-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       an official language in
       <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
        Wales
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the only
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">
         de jure
        </a>
       </i>
       official language in any part of the UK.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Approximately 1.5 million people in the UK speak
       <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">
        Scots
       </a>
       —although
       <a href="/wiki/Scots_language#Status" title="Scots language">
        there is debate
       </a>
       as to whether this is a distinct language, or a variety of English.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCOTSCENSUS_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SCOTSCENSUS-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.894_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.894-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is some discussion of the languages of the United Kingdom's three
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">
        Crown dependencies
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">
        Jersey
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">
        Guernsey
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
        Isle of Man
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       though they are not part of the United Kingdom.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          </span>
         </a>
         <ul>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2">
           <a href="#Living">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             1.1
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Living
            </span>
           </a>
           <ul>
            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-3">
             <a href="#Anglic">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_languages_and_dialects">
        List of languages and dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: List of languages and dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Living">
        Living
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Living">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The table below outlines living indigenous
       <b>
        languages of the United Kingdom
       </b>
       (England, Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland). The languages of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_Dependency" title="Crown Dependency">
        Crown Dependencies
       </a>
       (the
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">
        Channel Islands
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
        Isle of Man
       </a>
       ) are not included here.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable sortable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Language
        </th>
        <th>
         Type
        </th>
        <th>
         Spoken in
        </th>
        <th>
         No. of speakers in the UK
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
          English
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
          Germanic
         </a>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
          West Germanic
         </a>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         Throughout the United Kingdom
        </td>
        <td>
         59,824,194; 98% (2011 census)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnglishCensus_1-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-EnglishCensus-1">
           [1]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">
          Scots
         </a>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">
          Ulster Scots
         </a>
         in Northern Ireland)
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">
          Germanic
         </a>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
          West Germanic
         </a>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         Scotland (
         <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">
          Scottish Lowlands
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">
          Caithness
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Northern_Isles" title="Northern Isles">
          Northern Isles
         </a>
         )
         <br/>
         Northern Ireland (counties
         <a href="/wiki/County_Down" title="County Down">
          Down
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">
          Antrim
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/County_Londonderry" title="County Londonderry">
          Londonderry
         </a>
         ),
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berwick-on-Tweed" title="Berwick-on-Tweed">
          Berwick-on-Tweed
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         2.6% (2011 census)
         <ul>
          <li>
           <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
            Scotland
           </a>
           : 1,541,693; 30%
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scotscensus2_18-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-scotscensus2-18">
             [18]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </li>
          <li>
           <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">
            Northern Ireland
           </a>
           : 34,439; 2% (although 8.1% with some ability)
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ulsterscots_7-1">
            <a href="#cite_note-ulsterscots-7">
             [7]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
          Welsh
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">
          Celtic
         </a>
         (
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" title="Brythonic languages">
          Brythonic
         </a>
         )
        </td>
        <td>
         Wales (especially west and north) and parts of England near the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wales%E2%80%93England_border" title="Wales–England border">
          Welsh–English border
         </a>
         ; Welsh communities in major English cities such as London, Birmingham, Manchester and Liverpool.
        </td>
        <td>
         700,000; 1% (estimate)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WelshStatOverview_13-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-WelshStatOverview-13">
           [13]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <ul>
          <li>
           <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
            Wales
           </a>
           : 562,016; 19% (with 431,000, or 14.6%, considering themselves fluent)
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CensusWelsh_19-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-CensusWelsh-19">
             [19]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
            <a href="#cite_note-20">
             [20]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <br/>
           All skills (speaking, reading, or writing): 630,062
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
            <a href="#cite_note-21">
             [21]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </li>
          <li>
           <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
            England
           </a>
           : 110,000 (estimated speakers in 2001)
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WelshStatOverview_13-2">
            <a href="#cite_note-WelshStatOverview-13">
             [13]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <br/>
           8,200 first language speakers (2011 census)
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
            <a href="#cite_note-22">
             [22]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </li>
          <li>
           <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
            Scotland
           </a>
           and
           <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">
            Northern Ireland
           </a>
           : 1,000 (estimated speakers in 2001)
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WelshStatOverview_13-3">
            <a href="#cite_note-WelshStatOverview-13">
             [13]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/British_Sign_Language" title="British Sign Language">

       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       UK speakers, in the 2011 census, 59,824,194 (over the age of three) 98%.
      </p>
      <p>
       English is a
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic
       </a>
       language brought around the fifth century CE to the east coast of what is now
       <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
        England
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">
        Germanic-speaking
       </a>
       immigrants from around present-day northern Germany, who came to be known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">
        Anglo-Saxons
       </a>
       . The fusion of these settlers' dialects became what is now termed
       <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
        Old English
       </a>
       : the word
       <i>
        English
       </i>
       is derived from the name of the
       <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">
        Angles
       </a>
       . English soon
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celtic_language-death_in_England" title="Celtic language-death in England">
        displaced
       </a>
       the previously predominant
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Celtic" title="British Celtic">
        British Celtic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/British_Latin" title="British Latin">
        British Latin
       </a>
       throughout most of England. It spread into what was to become south-east
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       under the influence of the Anglian medieval
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Northumbria" title="Kingdom of Northumbria">
        kingdom of Northumbria
       </a>
       . Following the economic, political, military, scientific, cultural, and colonial influence of
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">
        Great Britain
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
        United Kingdom
       </a>
       from the eighteenth century, via the
       <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">
        British Empire
       </a>
       , and of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       since the mid-20th century, it has been widely dispersed around the world, become the leading language of international discourse. A significant number of English words are constructed based on roots from
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       , because Latin in some form was the lingua franca of the
       <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">
        Christian Church
       </a>
       and of European intellectual life. The language was further influenced by the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Old Norse
       </a>
       language with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">
        Viking
       </a>
       invasions in the 8th and 9th centuries. The
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">
        Norman conquest of England
       </a>
       in the 11th century gave rise to heavy borrowings from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_French" title="Norman French">
        Norman French
       </a>
       , and vocabulary and spelling conventions began to give the superficial appearance of a close relationship with Romance languages to what had now become
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">
        Middle English
       </a>
       . The Great Vowel Shift that began in the south of England in the 15th century is one of the historical events marking the separation of Middle and Modern English.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Wales">
        Wales
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Wales">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
        Welsh language
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Wales" title="Languages of Wales">
        Languages of Wales
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="670" data-file-width="750" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg/220px-Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg/330px-Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg/440px-Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wales.cardiff.slow.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bilingual road markings near
         <a href="/wiki/Cardiff_Airport" title="Cardiff Airport">
          Cardiff Airport
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Glamorgan" title="Vale of Glamorgan">
          Vale of Glamorgan
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Welsh (Cymraeg) emerged in the 6th century from
       <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">
        Brittonic
       </a>
       , the common ancestor of Welsh,
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
        Breton
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish
       </a>
       , and the extinct language known as
       <a href="/wiki/Cumbric" title="Cumbric">
        Cumbric
       </a>
       . Welsh is thus a member of the
       <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">
        Brythonic
       </a>
       branch of the
       <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">
        Celtic languages
       </a>
       , and is spoken natively in
       <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
        Wales
       </a>
       and by some along the Welsh border in
       <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
        England
       </a>
       . There are also Welsh speakers in
       <a href="/wiki/Y_Wladfa" title="Y Wladfa">
        Y Wladfa
       </a>
       (The Colony),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">
        Welsh
       </a>
       settlement in
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       , which began in 1865 and is situated mainly along the coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Chubut_Province" title="Chubut Province">
        Chubut Province
       </a>
       in the south of
       <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">
        Patagonia
       </a>
       . Chubut estimates the number of
       <a href="/wiki/Patagonian_Welsh" title="Patagonian Welsh">
        Patagonian Welsh
       </a>
       speakers to be about 1,500, while other estimates put the number at 5,000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
        Welsh language
       </a>
       is officially protected by the
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Act_1993" title="Welsh Language Act 1993">
        Welsh Language Act 1993
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Wales_Act_1998" title="Government of Wales Act 1998">
        Government of Wales Act 1998
       </a>
       , and since 1998 it has been common, for example, for almost all British Government Departments to provide both printed documentation and official websites in both English and Welsh. Both the English and Welsh
       <a href="/wiki/Wales#Languages" title="Wales">
        languages have equal status in Wales
       </a>
       according to law. On 7 December 2010, the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">
        National Assembly for Wales
       </a>
       unanimously approved a set of measures to develop the use of the Welsh language within Wales.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnews2010_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbcnews2010-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On 9 February 2011, this measure received Royal Assent and was passed, thus making the Welsh language an
       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">
        officially recognised language
       </a>
       within Wales.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Board" title="Welsh Language Board">
        Welsh Language Board
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-board_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-board-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       indicated in 2004 that 553,000 people (19.7% of the population of Wales in households or communal establishments) were able to speak Welsh. Based on an alternative definition, there has been a 0.9 percentage point increase when compared with the 2001 census, and an increase of approximately 35,000 in absolute numbers within Wales. Welsh is therefore a growing language within Wales.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-board_37-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-board-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Of those 553,000 Welsh speakers, 57% (315,000) were considered by others to be fluent, and 477,000 people consider themselves fluent or "fair" speakers. 62% of speakers (340,000) claimed to speak the language daily, including 88% of fluent speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-board_37-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-board-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       However, there is some controversy over the actual number who speak Welsh: some statistics include people who have studied Welsh to
       <a href="/wiki/General_Certificate_of_Secondary_Education" title="General Certificate of Secondary Education">
        GCSE
       </a>
       standard, many of whom could not be regarded as fluent speakers of the language. Conversely, some first-language speakers may choose not to report themselves as such. These phenomena, also seen with other minority languages outside the UK, make it harder to establish an accurate and unbiased figure for how many people speak it fluently. Furthermore, no question about Welsh language ability was asked in the 2001 census outside Wales, thereby ignoring a considerable population of Welsh speakers – particularly concentrated in neighbouring English counties and in London and other large cities.
      </p>
      <p>
       Nevertheless, the 2011 census recorded a drop of Welsh speakers overall, from 582,000 in 2001 to 562,000 in 2011, despite an increase in the size of the population—a 2% drop (from 21% to 19%) in the proportion of Welsh speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An estimated 110,000 to 150,000 people in
       <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
        England
       </a>
       speak Welsh.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WelshStatOverview_13-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-WelshStatOverview-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Scotland">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gaelic">
        Gaelic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Gaelic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PartickPartaig.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1760" data-file-width="1168" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/PartickPartaig.jpg/120px-PartickPartaig.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/PartickPartaig.jpg/180px-PartickPartaig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/PartickPartaig.jpg/240px-PartickPartaig.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PartickPartaig.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bilingual sign (Scottish Gaelic and English) at
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partick_railway_station" title="Partick railway station">
          Partick railway station
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">
          Glasgow
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Scotland" title="Languages of Scotland">
        Languages of Scotland
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       is a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celtic_language" title="Celtic language">
        Celtic language
       </a>
       native to
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       . A member of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goidel" title="Goidel">
        Goidelic
       </a>
       branch of the Celtic languages, Scottish Gaelic, like Modern Irish and Manx, developed out of Middle Irish, and thus descends ultimately from Primitive Irish. Outside Scotland, a dialect of the language known as
       <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Gaelic" title="Canadian Gaelic">
        Canadian Gaelic
       </a>
       exists in
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Breton_Island" title="Cape Breton Island">
        Cape Breton Island
       </a>
       and isolated areas of the
       <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">
        Nova Scotia
       </a>
       mainland. This variety has around 2000 speakers, amounting to 1.3% of the population of
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Breton_Island" title="Cape Breton Island">
        Cape Breton Island
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011#2011_Census_for_Scotland" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">
        2011 census of Scotland
       </a>
       showed that a total of 57,375 people (1.1% of the Scottish population aged over three years old) in Scotland could speak Gaelic at that time, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">
        Outer Hebrides
       </a>
       being the main stronghold of the language. The census results indicate a decline of 1,275 Gaelic speakers from 2001. A total of 87,056 people in 2011 reported having some facility with Gaelic compared to 93,282 people in 2001, a decline of 6,226.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_Census_of_Scotland_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-2011_Census_of_Scotland-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despite this decline, revival efforts exist and the number of speakers of the language under age 20 has increased.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Gaelic language was given official recognition for the first time in Scotland in 2005, by the
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">
        Scottish Parliament
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Language_(Scotland)_Act_2005" title="Gaelic Language (Scotland) Act 2005">
         Gaelic Language (Scotland) Act 2005
        </a>
       </i>
       , which aims to promote the Gaelic language to a status "commanding equal respect" with English. However, this wording has no clear meaning in law, and was chosen to prevent the assumption that the Gaelic language is in any way considered to have "equal validity or parity of esteem with English".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A major limitation of the act, though, is that it does not constitute any form of recognition for the Gaelic language by the UK government, and UK public bodies operating in Scotland, as reserved bodies, are explicitly exempted from its provisions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Scots">
        Scots
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Scots">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Scots language originated from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northumbrian_Old_English" title="Northumbrian Old English">
        Northumbrian Old English
       </a>
       . The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" title="Anglo-Saxon">
        Anglo-Saxon
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Northumbria" title="Kingdom of Northumbria">
        Kingdom of Northumbria
       </a>
       stretched from south
       <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">
        Yorkshire
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">
        Firth of Forth
       </a>
       from where the Scottish elite continued the language shift northwards. Since there are no universally accepted criteria for distinguishing languages from dialects, scholars and other interested parties often disagree about the linguistic, historical and social status of
       <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">
        Scots
       </a>
       . Although a number of paradigms for distinguishing between languages and dialects do exist, these often render contradictory results. Focused
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broad_Scots" title="Broad Scots">
        broad Scots
       </a>
       is at one end of a bipolar linguistic continuum, with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Standard_English" title="Scottish Standard English">
        Scottish Standard English
       </a>
       at the other. Consequently, Scots is often regarded as one of the ancient varieties of English, but with its own distinct dialects. Alternatively Scots is sometimes treated as a distinct Germanic language, in the way
       <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">
        Norwegian
       </a>
       is closely linked to, yet distinct from,
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
        Danish
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The 2011 UK census was the first to ask residents of Scotland about Scots. A campaign called Aye Can was set up to help individuals answer the question.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The specific wording used was "Which of these can you do? Tick all that apply" with options for 'Understand', 'Speak', 'Read' and 'Write' in three columns: English, Scottish Gaelic and Scots.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Of approximately 5.1 million respondents, about 1.2 million (24%) could speak, read and write Scots, 3.2 million (62%) had no skills in Scots and the remainder had some degree of skill, such as understanding Scots (0.27 million, 5.2%) or being able to speak it but not read or write it (0.18 million, 3.5%).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There were also small numbers of Scots speakers recorded in England and Wales on the 2011 Census, with the largest numbers being either in bordering areas (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Cumbria" title="Carlisle, Cumbria">
        Carlisle
       </a>
       ) or in areas that had recruited large numbers of Scottish workers in the past (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Corby" title="Corby">
        Corby
       </a>
       or the former mining areas of
       <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">
        Kent
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland">
        Northern Ireland
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Northern Ireland">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Languages of Northern Ireland">
        Languages of Northern Ireland
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_gaelic" title="Irish gaelic">
        Irish gaelic
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Irish" title="Ulster Irish">
        Ulster Irish
       </a>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ulster_Scots">
        Ulster Scots
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ulster Scots">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       2% speak
       <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">
        Ulster Scots
       </a>
       , seen by some as a language distinct from English and by some as a dialect of English, according to the 1999 Northern Ireland Life and Times Survey (around 30,000 speakers). Some definitions of Ulster Scots may also include Standard
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       spoken with an Ulster Scots accent. The language was brought to Ireland by
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scottish
       </a>
       planters from the 16th Century.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Irish_Gaelic_.28Gaeilge.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Irish_Gaelic_(Gaeilge)">
        Irish Gaelic (Gaeilge)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Irish Gaelic (Gaeilge)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Irish was the predominant language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">
        Irish people
       </a>
       for most of their recorded history, and they brought their Gaelic speech with them to other countries, notably
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
        Isle of Man
       </a>
       where it gave rise to
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">
        Manx
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       It has been estimated that the active Irish-language scene probably comprises 5 to 10 per cent of
       <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">
        Ireland
       </a>
       's population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 2011 census, 11% of the population of Northern Ireland claimed "some knowledge of Irish"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Census_2011_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Census_2011-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 3.7% reported being able to "speak, read, write and understand"
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
        Irish
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Census_2011_51-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Census_2011-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In another survey, from 1999, 1% of respondents said they spoke it as their main language at home.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Survey-NorthernIreland_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Survey-NorthernIreland-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Alongside British Sign Language,
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sign_Language" title="Irish Sign Language">
        Irish Sign Language
       </a>
       is also used.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cornwall">
        Cornwall
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cornwall">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish language
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Cornwall" title="Languages of Cornwall">
        Languages of Cornwall
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Kernowek
       </i>
       ), a Brythonic Celtic language closely related to Welsh, was the dominant language of
       <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">
        Cornwall
       </a>
       throughout the Middle Ages but began to decline after the 14th century, especially after the
       <a href="/wiki/Prayer_Book_Rebellion" title="Prayer Book Rebellion">
        Prayer Book Rebellion
       </a>
       in 1549. The language continued to function as the native language of
       <a href="/wiki/Penwith" title="Penwith">
        Penwith
       </a>
       in the far west of Cornwall until the late 18th century. The Cornish language was also used by some degree by fishermen in Mousehole (Porthynys) right up to the 1940s.
      </p>
      <p>
       Today it is spoken to some extent by roughly 3,500 people as a result of a revival initiated by
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Jenner" title="Henry Jenner">
        Henry Jenner
       </a>
       in 1903. Since 2002 the
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish language
       </a>
       has been recognised by the United Kingdom government as a UK official minority language under the
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">
        Council of Europe
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">
        European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Cornish Language Strategy project commissioned research to provide quantitative and qualitative evidence for the number of Cornish speakers: due to the success of the revival project it was estimated that 2,000 people were fluent (surveyed in spring 2008), an increase from the estimated 300 people who spoke Cornish fluently suggested in a study by Kenneth MacKinnon in 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walesonline_creche_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walesonline_creche-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Principal_minority_language_areas">
        Principal minority language areas
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Principal minority language areas">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Welsh:
        <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">
         North-West Wales
        </a>
        : Percentage of speakers 69% (76% understand Welsh). Population:
        <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">
         Gwynedd
        </a>
        - 118,400 (2001 census)
       </li>
       <li>
        Scottish Gaelic:
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Na_h-Eileanan_Siar" title="Na h-Eileanan Siar">
         Outer Hebrides
        </a>
        : Percentage of speakers 61%. Population:
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Na_h-Eileanan_Siar" title="Na h-Eileanan Siar">
         Na h-Eileanan Siar
        </a>
        - 27,400
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gro-scotland.gov.uk_56-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-gro-scotland.gov.uk-56">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In the 2001 census, each island overall was over 50% Gaelic speaking –
        <a href="/wiki/South_Uist" title="South Uist">
         South Uist
        </a>
        (71%),
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harris,_Outer_Hebrides" title="Harris, Outer Hebrides">
         Harris
        </a>
        (69%),
        <a href="/wiki/Barra" title="Barra">
         Barra
        </a>
        (68%),
        <a href="/wiki/North_Uist" title="North Uist">
         North Uist
        </a>
        (67%),
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isle_of_Lewis" title="Isle of Lewis">
         Lewis
        </a>
        (56%) and
        <a href="/wiki/Benbecula" title="Benbecula">
         Benbecula
        </a>
        (56%). With 59.3% of Gaelic speakers or a total of 15,723 speakers, this made the Outer Hebrides the most strongly coherent Gaelic speaking area in Scotland.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macpp_57-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Macpp-57">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
         <a href="#cite_note-58">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Jèrriais: Parish of
        <a href="/wiki/Saint_Ouen,_Jersey" title="Saint Ouen, Jersey">
         Saint Ouen
        </a>
        : Percentage of speakers: 8%. Population: Parish of Saint Ouen - 3,800 (4097 per 2011 census)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="British_Sign_Language">
        British Sign Language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: British Sign Language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/British_Sign_Language" title="British Sign Language">
        British Sign Language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       British Sign Language, often abbreviated to BSL, is the language of 125,000 Deaf adults, about 0.3%
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of the total population of the United Kingdom. It is not exclusively the language of Deaf people; many relatives of Deaf people and others can communicate in it fluently. Recognised to be a language by the UK Government on 18 March 2003,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hansard-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       BSL has the highest number of monolingual users of any indigenous minority language in the UK.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Second_or_additional_languages">
        Second or additional languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Second or additional languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Throughout the UK, many citizens can speak, or at least understand (to a degree where they could have a conversation with someone who speaks that language),
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="What is the difference between 'speak' and 'understand to a degree where they could have a conversation'? (December 2014)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       a second or even a third language from secondary school education, primary school education or from private classes. 23% of the UK population can speak/understand French, 9% can speak/understand German and 8% can speak/understand Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In general, 38% of UK citizens report that they can speak (well enough to have a conversation) at least one language other than their mother tongue, 18% at least two languages and 6% at least three languages. 62% of UK citizens cannot speak any second language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_8-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These figures include those who describe their level of ability in the second language as "basic".
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2013)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="UK_census">

            <div class="center">
             <div class="floatnone">
              <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" title="Scots">
               <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="14790" data-file-width="9970" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/219px-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/329px-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/438px-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 2x" width="219"/>
              </a>
             </div>
            </div>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr style="vertical-align:top">
           <td style="display:block;font-size:94%;padding:0px">

            </div>
