      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Status">
        Status
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Status">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Certain nations and regions of the UK have frameworks for the promotion of their
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autochthonous_language" title="Autochthonous language">
        autochthonous languages
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        In
        <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
         Wales
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Act_1993" title="Welsh Language Act 1993">
         Welsh Language Act 1993
        </a>
        requires English and
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
         Welsh
        </a>
        to be treated equally throughout the public sector. This was further enforced through the passing of the Welsh Language (Wales) Measure 2011.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
         <a href="#cite_note-64">
          [64]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
         <a href="#cite_note-65">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        In
        <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
         Scotland
        </a>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Language_(Scotland)_Act_2005" title="Gaelic Language (Scotland) Act 2005">
         Gaelic Language (Scotland) Act 2005
        </a>
        gave the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" title="Scottish Gaelic language">
         Scottish Gaelic language
        </a>
        its first statutory basis; and the
        <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">
         Western Isles
        </a>
        region of Scotland has a policy to promote the language.
       </li>
       <li>
        In
        <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">
         Northern Ireland
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Irish_language_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Irish language in Northern Ireland">
         Irish
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_language" title="Ulster Scots language">
         Ulster Scots
        </a>
        enjoy limited use alongside English (mainly in publicly commissioned translations).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The UK government has ratified the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">
        European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages
       </a>
       in respect of:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Cornish (in Cornwall)
       </li>
       <li>
        Irish and Ulster Scots (in Northern Ireland)
       </li>
       <li>
        Scots and Scottish Gaelic (in Scotland)
       </li>
       <li>
        Welsh (in Wales)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Under the European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages (which is not legally enforceable, but which requires states to adopt appropriate legal provision for the use of regional and minority languages) the UK government has committed itself to the recognition of certain regional languages and the promotion of certain linguistic traditions. The UK has ratified
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-r1-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       for the higher level of protection (Section III) provided for by the Charter in respect of Welsh, Scottish Gaelic and
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
        Irish
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">
        Scots
       </a>
       in Scotland and Northern Ireland (in the latter territory officially known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_language" title="Ulster Scots language">
        Ulster Scots
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ullans" title="Ullans">
        Ullans
       </a>
       , but in the speech of users simply as Scottish or Scots) are protected by the lower level only (Section II). The UK government has also recognised
       <a href="/wiki/British_Sign_Language" title="British Sign Language">
        British Sign Language
       </a>
       as a language in its own right
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard_59-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-hansard-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of the United Kingdom.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="869" data-file-width="962" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg/220px-Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg/330px-Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg/440px-Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         In Northern Ireland, the department responsible for culture displays official administrative identity in English, Irish and Ulster Scots
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A number of bodies have been established to oversee the promotion of the regional languages: in Scotland,
       <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B2rd_na_G%C3%A0idhlig" title="Bòrd na Gàidhlig">
        Bòrd na Gàidhlig
       </a>
       oversees Scottish Gaelic.
       <a href="/wiki/Foras_na_Gaeilge" title="Foras na Gaeilge">
        Foras na Gaeilge
       </a>
       has an all-Ireland remit as a cross-border language body, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tha_Boord_o_Ulst%C3%A8r-Scotch" title="Tha Boord o Ulstèr-Scotch">
        Tha Boord o Ulstèr-Scotch
       </a>
       is intended to fulfil a similar function for Ulster Scots, although hitherto it has mainly concerned itself with culture. In Wales, the Welsh Language Commissioner (Comisiynydd y Gymraeg) is an independent body established to promote and facilitate use of the Welsh language, mainly by imposing Welsh language standards on organisations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_Language_Partnership" title="Cornish Language Partnership">
        Cornish Language Partnership
       </a>
       is a body that represents the major
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish language
       </a>
       and cultural groups and local government's language needs. It receives funding from the UK government and the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
        European Union
       </a>
       , and is the regulator of the language's
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Written_Form" title="Standard Written Form">
        Standard Written Form
       </a>
       , agreed in 2008.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">
        Controversies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Controversies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_vs_dialect">
        Language vs dialect
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Language vs dialect">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       There are no universally accepted criteria for distinguishing
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">
         languages
        </a>
       </i>
       from
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
         dialects
        </a>
       </i>
       , although a number of paradigms exist, which give sometimes contradictory results. The distinction is therefore a subjective one, dependent on the user's frame of reference.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
        Irish
       </a>
       are generally viewed as being languages in their own right rather than dialects of a single tongue, but they are sometimes
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" title="Mutually intelligible">
        mutually intelligible
       </a>
       to a limited degree – especially between southern dialects of Scottish and northern dialects of Irish (programmes in these two forms of Gaelic are broadcast respectively on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_nan_Gaidheal" title="BBC Radio nan Gaidheal">
        BBC Radio nan Gaidheal
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/RT%C3%89_Raidi%C3%B3_na_Gaeltachta" title="RTÉ Raidió na Gaeltachta">
        RTÉ Raidió na Gaeltachta
       </a>
       ), but the relationship between Scots and English is less clear, since there is usually partial mutual intelligibility.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since there is a very high level of mutual intelligibility between contemporary speakers of Scots in Scotland and in Ulster (
       <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">
        Ulster Scots
       </a>
       ), and a common written form was current well into the 20th century, the two varieties have usually been considered as dialects of a single tongue rather than languages in their own right; the written forms have diverged in the 21st century. The government of the United Kingdom "recognises that Scots and Ulster Scots meet the Charter's definition of a regional or minority language".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_66-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-r1-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Whether this implies recognition of one regional or minority language or two is a question of interpretation. Ulster Scots is defined in legislation (The North/South Co-operation (Implementation Bodies) Northern Ireland Order 1999) as:
       <i>
        the variety of the Scots language which has traditionally been used in parts of Northern Ireland and in Donegal in Ireland
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While in
       <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">
        continental Europe
       </a>
       closely related languages and dialects may get official recognition and support, in the UK there is a tendency to view closely related
       <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">
        vernaculars
       </a>
       as a single language. Even British Sign Language is mistakenly thought of as a form of 'English' by some, rather than as a language in its own right, with a distinct
       <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">
        grammar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">
        vocabulary
       </a>
       . The boundaries are not always clear cut, which makes it hard to estimate numbers of speakers.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hostility">
        Hostility
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hostility">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2000" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg/220px-Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg/330px-Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg/440px-Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unidentified_irish_mural.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Mural in
         <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">
          Belfast
         </a>
         with
         <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
          Irish
         </a>
         phrase
         <i>
          Slán Abhaile
         </i>
         ("Safe home") directed ironically at departing
         <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">
          British soldiers
         </a>
         . In modern Northern Ireland, the Irish language is closely tied with
         <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">
          Irish republicanism
         </a>
         , despite being used historically by many Protestant and unionist communities.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In Northern Ireland, the use of Irish and Ulster Scots is sometimes viewed as politically loaded, despite both having been used by all communities in the past. According to the Northern Ireland Life and Times Survey 1999, the ratio of Unionist to Nationalist users of Ulster Scots is 2:1. About 1% of Catholics claim to speak it, while 2% of Protestants claim to speak it. The disparity in the ratios as determined by political and faith community, despite the very large overlap between the two, reflects the very low numbers of respondents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Across the two communities 0% speak it as their main language at home.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A 2:1 ratio would not differ markedly from that among the general population in those areas of Northern Ireland where Scots is spoken.
      </p>
      <p>
       Often the use of the
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_language_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Irish language in Northern Ireland">
        Irish language in Northern Ireland
       </a>
       has met with the considerable suspicion of Unionists, who have associated it with the largely Catholic
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">
        Republic of Ireland
       </a>
       , and more recently, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">
        republican movement
       </a>
       in Northern Ireland itself. Catholic areas of Belfast have street signs in Irish similar to those in the Republic. Approximately 14% of the population speak Irish,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       however only 1% speak it as their main language at home.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Under the
       <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews_Agreement" title="St Andrews Agreement">
        St Andrews Agreement
       </a>
       , the British government committed itself to introducing an Irish Language Act, and it was hoped that a consultation period ending on 2 March 2007 could see Irish becoming an official language, having equal validity with English, recognised as an indigenous language, or aspire to become an official language in the future.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, with the restoration of the
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">
        Northern Ireland Assembly
       </a>
       in May 2007, responsibility for this was passed to the Assembly, and the commitment was promptly broken. In October 2007, the then
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Culture,_Arts_and_Leisure" title="Department of Culture, Arts and Leisure">
        Minister of Culture, Arts and Leisure
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Poots" title="Edwin Poots">
        Edwin Poots
       </a>
       MLA, announced to the assembly that no Irish Language Act would be brought forward. As of April 2016, no Irish Language Act applying to Northern Ireland has been passed, and none is currently planned.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some resent Scottish Gaelic being promoted in the Lowlands. Gaelic place names are relatively rare in the extreme south-east (that part of Scotland which had previously been under Northumbrian rule)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the extreme north-east (part of
       <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">
        Caithness
       </a>
       , where
       <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">
        Norse
       </a>
       was previously spoken).
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2008)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Two areas with mostly Norse-derived placenames (and some Pictish), the Northern Isles (
       <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">
        Shetland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">
        Orkney
       </a>
       ) were ceded to Scotland in lieu of an unpaid dowry in 1472, and never spoke Gaelic; its traditional vernacular
       <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">
        Norn
       </a>
       , a derivative of Old Norse mutually intelligible with Icelandic and Faroese, died out in the 18th century after large-scale immigration by Lowland Scots speakers. To this day, many Shetlanders and Orcadians maintain a separate identity, albeit through the Shetlandic and Orcadian dialects of Lowland Scots, rather than their former tongue. Norn was also spoken at one point in
       <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">
        Caithness
       </a>
       , apparently dying out much earlier than Shetland and Orkney. However, the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Norse
       </a>
       speaking population were entirely assimilated by the
       <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">
        Gaelic
       </a>
       speaking population in the Western Isles; to what degree this happened in
       <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">
        Caithness
       </a>
       is a matter of controversy, although
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Gaelic
       </a>
       was spoken in parts of the county until the 20th century.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-recognition">
        Non-recognition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Non-recognition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Scots within Scotland and the regional varieties of English within England receive little or no official recognition. The dialects of northern England share some features with Scots that those of southern England do not. The regional dialects of England were once extremely varied, as is recorded in
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wright_(linguist)" title="Joseph Wright (linguist)">
        Joseph Wright
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Dialect_Dictionary" title="English Dialect Dictionary">
         English Dialect Dictionary
        </a>
       </i>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Survey_of_English_Dialects" title="Survey of English Dialects">
        Survey of English Dialects
       </a>
       , but they have died out over time so that regional differences are now largely in pronunciation rather than in grammar or vocabulary.
      </p>
      <p>
       Public funding of minority languages continues to produce mixed reactions, and there is sometimes resistance to their teaching in schools. Partly as a result, proficiency in languages other than "Standard" English can vary widely.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Immigrant_languages">
        Immigrant languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Immigrant languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Southall_station_sign.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Southall_station_sign.jpg/220px-Southall_station_sign.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Southall_station_sign.jpg/330px-Southall_station_sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Southall_station_sign.jpg/440px-Southall_station_sign.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Southall_station_sign.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Sign in English and Punjabi at
         <a href="/wiki/Southall_railway_station" title="Southall railway station">
          Southall railway station
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Southall" title="Southall">
          Southall
         </a>
         , London
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Duke_Streets_Liverpool_Chinatown.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="912" data-file-width="1308" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nelson_Duke_Streets_Liverpool_Chinatown.jpg/220px-Nelson_Duke_Streets_Liverpool_Chinatown.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nelson_Duke_Streets_Liverpool_Chinatown.jpg/330px-Nelson_Duke_Streets_Liverpool_Chinatown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Nelson_Duke_Streets_Liverpool_Chinatown.jpg/440px-Nelson_Duke_Streets_Liverpool_Chinatown.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nelson_Duke_Streets_Liverpool_Chinatown.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bilingual street signs in
         <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Liverpool" title="Chinatown, Liverpool">
          Chinatown, Liverpool
         </a>
         , Merseyside
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brick_Lane_street_signs.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1131" data-file-width="1886" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Brick_Lane_street_signs.JPG/220px-Brick_Lane_street_signs.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Brick_Lane_street_signs.JPG/330px-Brick_Lane_street_signs.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Brick_Lane_street_signs.JPG/440px-Brick_Lane_street_signs.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brick_Lane_street_signs.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         '
         <a href="/wiki/Brick_Lane" title="Brick Lane">
          Brick Lane
         </a>
         ' street sign in English and Bengali,
         <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Tower_Hamlets" title="London Borough of Tower Hamlets">
          Tower Hamlets
         </a>
         , London
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Communities migrating to the UK in recent decades have brought many more languages to the country. Surveys started in 1979 by the
       <a href="/wiki/Inner_London_Education_Authority" title="Inner London Education Authority">
        Inner London Education Authority
       </a>
       discovered over 100 languages being spoken domestically by the families of the inner city's school children.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Asians_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="South Asians in the United Kingdom">
        South Asians in the United Kingdom
       </a>
       speak dozens of different languages, and it is difficult to determine how many people speak each language alongside English. The largest subgroup of British Asians are those of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjabi_people" title="Punjabi people">
        Punjabi
       </a>
       origin (representing approximately two thirds of direct migrants from South Asia to the UK), from both India and Pakistan, they number over 2 million in the UK and are the largest Punjabi community outside of South Asia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">
        Punjabi language
       </a>
       is currently the third most spoken language in the UK. Many
       <a href="/wiki/Black_British" title="Black British">
        Black Britons
       </a>
       speak English as their first language. Their ancestors mostly came from
       <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">
        the West Indies
       </a>
       , particularly Jamaica, and generally also spoke English-based creole languages,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       hence there are significant numbers of Caribbean creole speakers (see below for Ethnologue figures). With over 300,000
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Briton" title="French Briton">
        French
       </a>
       -born people in the UK, plus the general popularity of the language, French is understood by 23% of the country's population.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       A large proportion of the
       <a href="/wiki/Black_British" title="Black British">
        Black British
       </a>
       population, especially African-born immigrants speak French as a first or second language.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">
        Bengali
       </a>
       speaking community in the UK consists of those largely of
       <a href="/wiki/British_Bangladeshi" title="British Bangladeshi">
        Bangladeshi origin
       </a>
       mainly from the
       <a href="/wiki/Sylhet_Division" title="Sylhet Division">
        Sylhet
       </a>
       region (predominantly
       <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
        Muslim
       </a>
       ), and small numbers of Indians from the
       <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">
        West Bengal
       </a>
       region (mainly
       <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">
        Hindu
       </a>
       ). There are around 700,000 Bengali speakers, 550,000 of whom speak
       <a href="/wiki/Sylheti_language" title="Sylheti language">
        Sylheti
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologue_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ethnologue-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which is either considered a dialect of Bengali or as a separate language. West Bengalis mainly speak the standard Bengali language, whereas Bangladeshis mainly speak Sylheti, which is generally not written, although children may receive some education in standard Bengali at school. Sylheti is not recognised as a language in Bangladesh, and there some debate to whether it should be recognised as a language separate from Bengali. The Bengali speaking community in the UK is highly concentrated in the
       <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Tower_Hamlets" title="London Borough of Tower Hamlets">
        London Borough of Tower Hamlets
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Most_common_immigrant_languages">
        Most common immigrant languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Most common immigrant languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       According to the 2011 census, English or Welsh was the main language of 92.3% of the residents of England and Wales. Among other languages, the most common were as follows.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">
         Polish
        </a>
        546,000 or 1.0%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Punjabi language in the United Kingdom">
         Punjabi
        </a>
        273,000 or 0.5%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdu_language_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Urdu language in the United Kingdom">
         Urdu
        </a>
        269,000 or 0.5%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">
         Bengali
        </a>
        (with
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sylheti" title="Sylheti">
         Sylheti
        </a>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chatgaya" title="Chatgaya">
         Chatgaya
        </a>
        ) 221,000 or 0.4%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">
         Gujarati
        </a>
        213,000 or 0.4%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
         Arabic
        </a>
        159,000 or 0.3%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
         French
        </a>
        147,000 or 0.3%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
         Chinese
        </a>
        141,000 or 0.3%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
         Portuguese
        </a>
        133,000 or 0.2%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
         Spanish
        </a>
        120,000 or 0.2%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">
         Tamil
        </a>
        101,000 or 0.2%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">
         Turkish
        </a>
        99,000 or 0.2%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
         Italian
        </a>
        92,000 or 0.2%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">
         Somali
        </a>
        86,000 or 0.2%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">
         Lithuanian
        </a>
        85,000 or 0.2%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
         German
        </a>
        77,000 or 0.1%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">
         Persian
        </a>
        76,000 or 0.1%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
         Philippine languages
        </a>
        (with
        <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
         Tagalog
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Cebuano_language" title="Cebuano language">
         Cebuano
        </a>
        ) 70,000 or 0.1%
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
         Romanian
        </a>
        68,000 or 0.1%
       </li>
      </ol>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Norman_French_and_Latin">
        Norman French and Latin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Norman French and Latin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/220px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/330px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/440px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The signs at
         <a href="/wiki/Wallsend_Metro_station" title="Wallsend Metro station">
          Wallsend Metro station
         </a>
         are in English and Latin as a tribute to Wallsend's role as one of the outposts of the Roman empire.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Law_French" title="Law French">
        Norman French
       </a>
       is still used in the
       <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">
        Houses of Parliament
       </a>
       for certain official business between the clerks of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" title="British House of Commons">
        House of Commons
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">
        House of Lords
       </a>
       , and on other official occasions such as the dissolution of Parliament.
      </p>
      <p>
       Latin is also used to a limited degree in certain official mottoes, for example
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nemo_me_impune_lacessit" title="Nemo me impune lacessit">
         Nemo me impune lacessit
        </a>
       </i>
       , legal terminology (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">
         habeas corpus
        </a>
       </i>
       ), and various ceremonial contexts. Latin abbreviations can also be seen on British coins. The use of Latin has declined greatly in recent years. However, the
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic Church
       </a>
       retains Latin in official and quasi-official contexts. Latin remains the language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rite" title="Roman Rite">
        Roman Rite
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Tridentine_Mass" title="Tridentine Mass">
        Tridentine Mass
       </a>
       is celebrated in Latin. Although the
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_of_Paul_VI" title="Mass of Paul VI">
        Mass of Paul VI
       </a>
       is usually celebrated in English, it can be and often is said in Latin, in part or whole, especially at multilingual gatherings. It is the official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">
        Holy See
       </a>
       , the primary language of its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_journal" title="Public journal">
        public journal
       </a>
       , the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Acta_Apostolicae_Sedis" title="Acta Apostolicae Sedis">
         Acta Apostolicae Sedis
        </a>
       </i>
       , and the working language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rota" title="Roman Rota">
        Roman Rota
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At one time, Latin and Greek were commonly taught in British schools (and were required for entrance to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_universities" title="Ancient universities">
        ancient universities
       </a>
       until 1919, for Greek, and the 1960s, for Latin
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), and A-Levels and Highers are still available in both subjects.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Languages_of_the_Channel_Islands_and_Isle_of_Man">
        Languages of the Channel Islands and Isle of Man
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Languages of the Channel Islands and Isle of Man">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
        Isle of Man
       </a>
       and the Bailiwicks of
       <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">
        Guernsey
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">
        Jersey
       </a>
       are not part of the UK, but are closely associated with it, being
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Crown_Dependencies" title="British Crown Dependencies">
        British Crown Dependencies
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       For the insular forms of English, see
       <a href="/wiki/Manx_English" title="Manx English">
        Manx English
       </a>
       (Anglo-Manx),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guernsey_English" title="Guernsey English">
        Guernsey English
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_Island_English" title="Channel Island English">
        Jersey English
       </a>
       . Forms of
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       are, or have been, used as an official language in the
       <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">
        Channel Islands
       </a>
       , e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Jersey_Legal_French" title="Jersey Legal French">
        Jersey Legal French
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The indigenous languages of the Crown dependencies are recognised as regional languages by the British and Irish governments within the framework of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British-Irish_Council" title="British-Irish Council">
        British-Irish Council
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Guernsey">
        Guernsey
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Guernsey">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Guern%C3%A9siais" title="Guernésiais">
        Guernésiais
       </a>
       , a form of
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       spoken on
       <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">
        Guernsey
       </a>
       , is related to
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">
        Norman
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O%C3%AFl_languages" title="Oïl languages">
        Oïl
       </a>
       languages. 14% of the population claim some understanding of the language.
      </p>
      <p>
       There is intercomprehension (with some difficulty) with Jèrriais-speakers from Jersey and Norman-speakers from mainland Normandy. Guernésiais most closely resembles the Norman dialect of La Hague in the Cotentin Peninsula (Cotentinais). The creation of a Guernsey Language Commission was announced on 7 February 2013
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as an initiative by government to preserve the linguistic culture. The Commission has operated since Liberation Day, 9 May 2013.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Jersey">
        Jersey
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Jersey">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A8rriais" title="Jèrriais">
        Jèrriais
       </a>
       , a form of
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       spoken on
       <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">
        Jersey
       </a>
       , is related to
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">
        Norman
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/O%C3%AFl_languages" title="Oïl languages">
        Oïl
       </a>
       ) languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although
       <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A8rriais" title="Jèrriais">
        Jèrriais
       </a>
       is now the first language of a very small minority, until the 19th century it was the everyday language of the majority of the population, and even until the Second World War up to half the population could communicate in the language. The use of Jèrriais is also to be noted during the
       <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_the_Channel_Islands" title="German occupation of the Channel Islands">
        German occupation of the Channel Islands
       </a>
       during the Second World War; the local population used Jèrriais among themselves as a language neither the occupying Germans, nor their French interpreters, could understand. However, the social and economic upheaval of the War meant that use of English increased dramatically after the Liberation. It is considered that the last monolingual adult speakers probably died in the 1950s, although monolingual speaking children were being received into schools in St. Ouen as late as the late 1970s.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Sercquiais" title="Sercquiais">
        Sercquiais
       </a>
       dialect of
       <a href="/wiki/Sark" title="Sark">
        Sark
       </a>
       is descended from Jèrriais, but is not recognised under this framework and the last native speaker died in 2004.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Auregnais" title="Auregnais">
        Auregnais
       </a>
       , the Norman dialect of
       <a href="/wiki/Alderney" title="Alderney">
        Alderney
       </a>
       , is now extinct.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Isle_of_Man">
        Isle of Man
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Isle of Man">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">
        Manx
       </a>
       began to diverge from Early Modern Irish in around the 13th century and from
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       in the 15th. The language sharply declined during the 19th century and was supplanted by English. The UK government has ratified the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">
        European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages
       </a>
       on behalf of the Manx government.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although only a small minority of the
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
        Isle of Man
       </a>
       's population is fluent in the language, but a larger minority has some knowledge of it. Manx is widely considered to be an important part of the island's culture and heritage. Although the last surviving
       <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">
        native speaker
       </a>
       of the Manx language,
       <a href="/wiki/Ned_Maddrell" title="Ned Maddrell">
        Ned Maddrell
       </a>
       , died in 1974, the language has never fallen completely out of use. Manx has been the subject of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language_revival" title="Language revival">
        language revival
       </a>
       efforts, so that despite the small number of speakers, Manx has become more visible on the island, with increased signage, radio broadcasts and a bi-lingual Primary School. The revival of Manx has been aided by the fact that the language was well recorded; for example, the Bible was translated into Manx, and audio recordings were made of native speakers. In the 2011 census, 1,823 out of 80,398, or 2.27% of the population, claimed to have knowledge of Manx.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gov.im_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gov.im-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is an increase of 134 people from the 2001 census.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbc-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extinct_British_languages">
        Extinct British languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Extinct British languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Norn">
        Norn
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Norn">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">
        Norn language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic
       </a>
       once spoken in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands" title="Shetland Islands">
        Shetland Islands
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands" title="Orkney Islands">
        Orkney Islands
       </a>
       &amp;
       <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">
        Caithness
       </a>
       . The last reports of Norn speakers are claimed to be from the 19th century, but it is more likely that the language was dying out in the late 18th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The isolated islands of
       <a href="/wiki/Foula" title="Foula">
        Foula
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Unst" title="Unst">
        Unst
       </a>
       are variously claimed as the last refuges of the language in Shetland, where there were people "who could repeat sentences in Norn, probably passages from folk songs or poems, as late as 1893.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Walter_Sutherland_(Norn)" title="Walter Sutherland (Norn)">
        Walter Sutherland
       </a>
       from Skaw in Unst, who died about 1850, has been cited as the last native speaker of the Norn language. However, fragments of vocabulary survived the death of the main language and remain to this day, mainly in place-names and terms referring to plants, animals, weather, mood, and fishing vocabulary.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pictish">
        Pictish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Pictish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Pictish_language" title="Pictish language">
        Pictish language
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pictish" title="Pictish">
        Pictish
       </a>
       was probably a
       <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">
        Brittonic
       </a>
       language, or dialect, spoken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">
        Picts
       </a>
       , the people of northern and central
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">
        Early Middle Ages
       </a>
       , which became extinct c.900 AD. There is virtually no direct attestation of Pictish, short of a limited number of
       <a href="/wiki/Toponymy" title="Toponymy">
        geographical
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Anthroponymy" title="Anthroponymy">
        personal names
       </a>
       found on monuments and the contemporary records in the area controlled by the Kingdom of the Picts. Such evidence, however, points to the language being closely related to the
       <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">
        Brittonic language
       </a>
       spoken prior to
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">
        Anglo-Saxon settlement
       </a>
       in what is now southern Scotland, England and Wales. A minority view held by a few scholars claims that Pictish was at least partially non-Indo-European or that a non-Indo-European and Brittonic language coexisted.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cumbric">
        Cumbric
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Cumbric">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Cumbric" title="Cumbric">
        Cumbric
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Cumbric' was a
       <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
        variety
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">
        Common Brittonic
       </a>
       language spoken during the
       <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">
        Early Middle Ages
       </a>
       in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hen_Ogledd" title="Hen Ogledd">
         Hen Ogledd
        </a>
       </i>
       or "Old North" in what is now
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_England" title="Northern England">
        Northern England
       </a>
       and southern
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">
        Lowland
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CelticCulture_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CelticCulture-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was closely related to
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">
        Old Welsh
       </a>
       and the other
       <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">
        Brittonic languages
       </a>
       . Place name evidence suggests Cumbric speakers may have carried it into other parts of northern England as migrants from its core area further north.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It may also have been spoken as far south as
       <a href="/wiki/Pendle" title="Pendle">
        Pendle
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_Dales" title="Yorkshire Dales">
        Yorkshire Dales
       </a>
       . Most linguists think that it became extinct in the 12th century, after the incorporation of the semi-independent
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">
        Kingdom of Strathclyde
       </a>
       into the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">
        Kingdom of Scotland
       </a>
       .
      </p>
